    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Chromosome-level genome assembly, annotation and evolutionary analysis of the ornamental plant Asparagus setaceus"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-04-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Asparagus setaceus is a popular ornamental plant cultivated in tropical and subtropical regions globally. Here, we constructed a chromosome-scale reference genome of A. setaceus to facilitate the investigation of its genome characteristics and evolution. Using a combination of Nanopore long reads, Illumina short reads, 10&#215; Genomics linked reads, and Hi-C data, we generated a high-quality genome assembly of A. setaceus covering 710.15&#8201;Mb, accounting for 98.63% of the estimated genome size. A total of 96.85% of the sequences were anchored to ten superscaffolds corresponding to the ten chromosomes. The genome of A. setaceus was predicted to contain 28,410 genes, 25,649 (90.28%) of which were functionally annotated. A total of 65.59% of the genome was occupied by repetitive sequences, among which long terminal repeats were predominant (42.51% of the whole genome). Evolutionary analysis revealed an estimated divergence time of A. setaceus from its close relative A. officinalis of ~9.66 million years ago, and A. setaceus underwent two rounds of whole-genome duplication. In addition, 762 specific gene families, 96 positively selected genes, and 76 resistance (R) genes were detected and functionally predicted in A. setaceus. These findings provide new knowledge about the characteristics and evolution of the A. setaceus genome, and will facilitate comparative genetic and genomic research on the genus Asparagus."/>

Here, we constructed a chromosome-scale reference genome of A. setaceus to facilitate the investigation of its genome characteristics and evolution. Using a combination of Nanopore long reads, Illumina short reads, 10&#215; Genomics linked reads, and Hi-C data, we generated a high-quality genome assembly of A. setaceus covering 710.15&#8201;Mb, accounting for 98.63% of the estimated genome size.             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Asparagus setaceus</i> is a popular ornamental plant cultivated in tropical and subtropical regions globally. Here, we constructed a chromosome-scale reference genome of <i>A. setaceus</i> to facilitate the investigation of its genome characteristics and evolution. Using a combination of Nanopore long reads, Illumina short reads, 10Ã Genomics linked reads, and Hi-C data, we generated a high-quality genome assembly of <i>A. setaceus</i> covering 710.15âMb, accounting for 98.63% of the estimated genome size. A total of 96.85% of the sequences were anchored to ten superscaffolds corresponding to the ten chromosomes. The genome of <i>A. setaceus</i> was predicted to contain 28,410 genes, 25,649 (90.28%) of which were functionally annotated. A total of 65.59% of the genome was occupied by repetitive sequences, among which long terminal repeats were predominant (42.51% of the whole genome). Evolutionary analysis revealed an estimated divergence time of <i>A. setaceus</i> from its close relative <i>A. officinalis</i> of ~9.66 million years ago, and <i>A. setaceus</i> underwent two rounds of whole-genome duplication. In addition, 762 specific gene families, 96 positively selected genes, and 76 resistance (R) genes were detected and functionally predicted in <i>A. setaceus</i>. These findings provide new knowledge about the characteristics and evolution of the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome, and will facilitate comparative genetic and genomic research on the genus <i>Asparagus</i>.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>Asparagus</i> L. is a monocot genus belonging to Asparagaceae (Asparagales) that comprises &gt;200 species distributed widely in regions with an aridâsubarid climate in the Old World<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dahlgren, R. M. T. et al. (eds) The Families of the Monocotyledons (Springer-Verlag Press, 1985)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d14961e451">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Clifford, H. T. &amp; Conran, J. G. Asparagaceae. in Flora of Australia (ed George, A. S.) (Australian Government Publishing Service, 1987)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14961e451_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Kubituki, K. &amp; Rudall, P. J. Asparagaceae. in The Families and Genera of Vascular Plants (ed Kubituki, K.) (Springer-Verlag Press, 1998)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d14961e454">3</a></sup>. This genus includes commercially important vegetable species, most prominently <i>A. officinalis</i>, and some species with great ornamental and/or medicinal value, such as <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. cochinchinensis</i>. There are three subgenera in the <i>Asparagus</i> genus: <i>Asparagus</i>, <i>Myrsiphyllum</i>, and <i>Protasparagus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Clifford, H. T. &amp; Conran, J. G. Asparagaceae. in Flora of Australia (ed George, A. S.) (Australian Government Publishing Service, 1987)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14961e479">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Ellison, J. H. &amp; Kinelski, J. J. Greenwich, a male asparagus hybrid. HortScience 21, 1249 (1986)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14961e482">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Fukuda, T. et al. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) inferred from plastid petB intron and petD-rpoA intergenic spacer sequences. Plant Spec. Biol. 20, 121â132 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14961e485">5</a></sup>. Within the <i>Asparagus</i> subgenus, all species are dioecious, whereas the species in the <i>Myrsiphyllum</i> and <i>Protasparagus</i> subgenera are hermaphroditic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Clifford, H. T. &amp; Conran, J. G. Asparagaceae. in Flora of Australia (ed George, A. S.) (Australian Government Publishing Service, 1987)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14961e499">2</a></sup>. Although this genus has important commercial value, only the model dioecious plant <i>A. officinalis</i> has been extensively investigated, the including sequencing and assembly of a reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, S. F. et al. Analysis of transposable elements in the genome of Asparagus officinalis from high coverage sequence data. PLoS One 9, e97189 (2014)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14961e506">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, S. F. et al. Comparative transcriptome analysis reveals differentially expressed genes associated with sex expression in garden asparagus (Asparagus officinalis). BMC Plant Biol. 17, 143 (2017)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14961e506_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Harkess, A. et al. Sex-biased gene expression in dioecious garden asparagus (Asparagus officinalis). N. Phytol. 207, 883â892 (2015)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14961e506_2">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Murase, K. et al. MYB transcription factor gene involved in sex determination in Asparagus officinalis. Genes Cells 22, 115â123 (2016)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14961e506_3">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dong, T. et al. Anthocyanins accumulation and molecular analysis of correlated genes by metabolome and transcriptome in green and purple asparagus (Asparagus officinalis L.). Food Chem. 271, 18â28 (2019)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14961e506_4">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Harkess, A. et al. The asparagus genome sheds light on the origin and evolution of a young Y chromosome. Nat. Commun. 8, 1279 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14961e509">11</a></sup>. Other species, especially hermaphroditic species, are poorly investigated. The absence of a reference genome for hermaphroditic species has limited our understanding of the biology and evolution of the <i>Asparagus</i> genus.</p><p>Among hermaphroditic <i>Asparagus</i> species, <i>A. setaceus</i> (synonyms: <i>A. plumosus</i>, <i>Protasparagus plumosus</i>, and <i>P. setaceus</i>) is a scrambling perennial herb with needle-like fascicled cladodes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Pindel, A. Regeneration capacity of Asparagus setaceus (Kunth) Jessop âPyramidalisâ in in vitro cultures. Acta Sci. Pol. Hortorum Cultus 16, 85â93 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14961e535">12</a></sup>. It is a very popular ornamental plant because of its attractive traits of extremely feathery, soft leaves, and an elegant posture (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig1">1</a>). <i>A. setaceus</i> also has multiple uses in traditional oriental medicine<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="McGaw, L. J. &amp; Eloff, J. N. Ethnoveterinary use of southern African plants and scientific evaluation of their medicinal properties. J. Ethnopharmacol. 119, 559â574 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14961e545">13</a></sup>. As a wild relative species of the important vegetable <i>A. officinalis</i>, <i>A. setaceus</i> is resistant to purple spot disease caused by infection with <i>Stemphylium vesicarium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Bansal, R. K., Menzies, S. A. &amp; Broadhurst, P. G. Screening of Asparagus species for resistance to Stemphylium leaf spot. NZ J. Agric. Res. 29, 539â545 (1986)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14961e558">14</a></sup> and rust disease caused by infection with <i>Puccinia asparagi</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Fukuda, T. et al. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) inferred from plastid petB intron and petD-rpoA intergenic spacer sequences. Plant Spec. Biol. 20, 121â132 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14961e564">5</a></sup>, which are common pathogens of <i>A. officinalis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Alberti, P. et al. Interspecific hybridization for Asparagus breeding. In Proc of the XLVIII Italian Society of Agricultural Genetics-SIFV-SIGA Joint Meeting (Lecce, Italy, 2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14961e570">15</a></sup>. Investigating the mechanisms of agricultural characteristics related to pathogen resistance is potentially valuable for the molecular breeding of <i>A. officinalis</i>. Therefore, <i>A. setaceus</i> has high commercial and medicinal value, and is the subject of scientific research because of its properties. However, research on this species is limited. Only a few studies have explored the base chromosome number (2<i>n</i>â=â2<i>x</i>â=â20) and karyotype of <i>A. setaceus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Akter, S., Begum, K. N., Sultana, S. S. &amp; Alam, S. S. Karyotype diversity in three Asparagus L. species. Cytologia 82, 551â557 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14961e589">16</a></sup> and its genome size (~720âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Å tajner, N., Bohanec, B. &amp; Javornik, B. Genetic variability of economically important Asparagus species as revealed by genome size analysis and rDNA ITS polymorphisms. Plant Sci. 162, 931â937 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14961e593">17</a></sup>, micropropagation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Pindel, A. Regeneration capacity of Asparagus setaceus (Kunth) Jessop âPyramidalisâ in in vitro cultures. Acta Sci. Pol. Hortorum Cultus 16, 85â93 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14961e598">12</a></sup>, chloroplast genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Li, J. R. et al. Characterization of the complete chloroplast genome of Asparagus setaceus. Mitochondrial DNA B 4, 2639â2640 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d14961e602">18</a></sup>, and phylogenetic relationships with other <i>Asparagus</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kubota, S., Konno, I. &amp; Kannos, A. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) explains interspecific crossability between the garden asparagus (A. officinalis) and other Asparagus species. Theor. Appl. Genet. 124, 345â354 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14961e609">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Norup, M. F. et al. Evolution of Asparagus L. (Asparagaceae): Out-of-South-Africa and multiple origins of sexual dimorphism. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 92, 25â44 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14961e612">20</a></sup>. Genome sequence analysis can greatly promote molecular and genetic studies on this species, and the <i>A. officinalis</i>â<i>A. setaceus</i> genome pair provides a suitable model for the evolutionary analysis of the <i>Asparagus</i> genus.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="649" height="864" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Image of an <i>A. setaceus</i> plant</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In this study, we de novo assembled the genome of <i>A. setaceus</i> through a combination of sequencing strategies, including the use of Nanopore, Illumina, 10Ã Genomics, and Hi-C technology. Genome annotation, the characterization of genome features, evolutionary analysis, and resistance gene identification were performed based on the assembled genome sequences. Our results provide a foundation for further genome-wide studies on <i>A. setaceus</i> and will be useful for studying the evolution of the <i>Asparagus</i> genus.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>A single plant <i>A. setaceus</i>, which has ten homologous pairs of chromosomes in diploid cells (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig2">2</a>), was used for genome sequencing. The analysis of the 17-mer frequency revealed high genome heterozygosity of 1.9% (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><p>For accurate assembly in this highly heterozygous plant, we sequenced the genome by utilizing a combination of Illumina, Nanopore, 10Ã Genomics, and Hi-C approaches, and assembled the sequences by using a series of methods. The sequencing and genome assembly workflow is shown in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">2a</a>. We obtained a total of 112.52âGb of Nanopore long reads (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">2b</a>) corresponding to ~156.28Ã coverage of the ~720âMb <i>A. setaceus</i> genome, as estimated using flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Å tajner, N., Bohanec, B. &amp; Javornik, B. Genetic variability of economically important Asparagus species as revealed by genome size analysis and rDNA ITS polymorphisms. Plant Sci. 162, 931â937 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14961e682">17</a></sup>. The Nanopore long reads were assembled into contigs by de novo methods. The primary contigs were adjusted with Illumina paired-end reads (84.63âGb) and then employed for scaffold assembly using 10Ã Genomics data. The ~180âGb of 10Ã Genomics sequencing reads included 2615 contigs grouped into 2061 scaffolds. After redundancy was removed, the assembled genome included 1393 scaffolds with an N50 length of 2.19âMb. We further connected these scaffolds into superscaffolds by using Hi-C reads (~96âGb, 133-fold coverage; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">2c</a>). Ten of the largest superscaffolds exhibited a total length of 687.77âMb and matched the ten <i>A. setaceus</i> chromosomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">1</a>). The final assembly of the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome was 710.15âMb in length, constituting 98.63% of the predicted genome size. Among the obtained sequences, 96.85% were anchored to the ten chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig3">3</a>), whereas 22.38âMb in 657 scaffolds remained unmapped. The sequencing and assembly information are summarized in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Tab1">1</a>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Cytogenetic investigation of A. setaceus chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Cytogenetic investigation of <i>A. setaceus</i> chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="945" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Cytological analysis of <i>A. setaceus</i> chromosomes in root tip cells. <b>b</b> Karyotyping of <i>A. setaceus</i> chromosomes based on 45âS rDNA FISH. The 45âS rDNA was labeled with Chroma Tide Alexa Fluor 488 (green), and the chromosomes were counterstained with DAPI (blue). Scale bar, 10âÎ¼m</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Characterization of the A. setaceus genome. Ten pseudochromosomes were ordered by size."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Characterization of the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome. Ten pseudochromosomes were ordered by size.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="680" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>A: Gene density (brown); B: GC content (black); C: density of <i>Gypsy</i> retrotransposable elements (red); D: density of <i>Copia</i> retrotransposable elements (blue); and E: relationship between syntenic blocks, as indicated by lines</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the sequencing and assembly of the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Evaluation of the genome assembly</h3><p>The completeness of this assembled genome was assessed using BUSCO analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14961e861">21</a></sup>. Approximately 90.0% of the plant orthologs were included in the assembled sequences (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">2</a>). Furthermore, 89.85% of the transcriptome reads could be mapped to the assembled sequences. In the karyotype of <i>A. setaceus</i>, the first six pairs of chromosomes were clearly larger than the remaining chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig2">2</a>), and the assembly results were consistent with this observation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig3">3</a>). These results suggested a high accuracy and completeness of the genome assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Annotation of the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome assembly</h3><p>We combined different strategies to identify protein-coding genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">2d</a>). A total of 28,410 genes were identified in the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">3</a>). The average gene length was 6398âbp, and the mean exon number of each gene was 4.95 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">4</a>). Among these genes, 90.28% showed homology with known genes according to BLAST analysis (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">5</a>). In addition, 2126 noncoding RNAs, including 388 microRNAs (miRNAs), 784 tRNAs, 273 rRNAs, and 681 small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs), were detected (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>Complicated transposable element (TE) annotation showed that 64.43% of the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome assembly was comprised of TEs. Among these TEs, long terminal repeats were predominant, constituting ~42.51% of the assembled genome of <i>A. setaceus</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">7</a>). DNA transposons, long interspersed nuclear elements (LINEs), and short interspersed nuclear elements (SINEs) accounted for 4.12%, 2.90%, and 0.04% of the total assembly, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">7</a>). Simple sequence repeats (SSRs) are another type of important tandemly repetitive sequences. We used MISA software to detect SSRs in the genome of <i>A. setaceus</i>. A total of 215,955 SSRs (i.e., 85,131 mono-, 103,002 di-, 20,878 tri-, 3683 tetra-, 1967 penta-, and 1294 hexa-nucleotide repeats) were detected (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">8</a>). The total length of the SSR sequences was 8,914,237âbp, accounting for ~1.26% of the assembled <i>A. setaceus</i> genome. Thus, repetitive sequences, including TEs and SSRs, occupied 65.59% of the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome. The basic annotation information is listed in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Tab2">2</a>. The gene density, GC content, <i>Gypsy</i>, and <i>Copia</i> density mapped on the ten <i>A. setaceus</i> chromosomes are shown in a circos plot in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig3">3</a>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Annotation of the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome assembly</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Evolutionary analysis</h3><p>Evolutionary analysis was performed by comparing the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome with the genomes of 12 other representative plant species. These species included one other plant in the <i>Asparagus</i> genus (<i>A. officinalis</i>), one additional plant in the Asparagales order (<i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i>), three additional plants in the monocot clade (<i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i>, and <i>Musa acuminate</i>), and seven other representative plants in the eudicot clade (<i>Amborella trichopoda</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Spinacia oleracea</i>, and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>).</p><p>OrthoMCL gene family clustering analysis revealed a total of 13,355 gene families consisting of 21,981 genes in the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig4">4a</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">9</a>). OrthoMCL clustering recovered 1002 strictly single-copy ortholog gene sets among the 13 analyzed species. Ortholog analysis revealed that <i>A. setaceus</i>, <i>A. officinalis</i>, <i>C. papaya</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, and <i>A. trichopoda</i> shared a core set of 7905 gene families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig4">4b</a>). Further large-scale analysis among <i>A. setaceus</i> and the 12 other selected species showed that 762 gene families specific to <i>A. setaceus</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">9</a>). These specific genes are mostly involved in heme binding, DNA binding, oxidoreductase activity, and iron and zinc ion binding (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Gene family and phylogenetic analysis of A. setaceus and other representative plant genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Gene family and phylogenetic analysis of <i>A. setaceus</i> and other representative plant genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="501" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Clusters of orthologous and paralogous gene families in <i>A. setaceus</i> and 12 other sequenced plant genomes. <b>b</b> Venn diagram representing the distribution of shared gene families among <i>A. setaceus</i> and four other plants (<i>A. officinalis</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. papaya</i>, and <i>A. trichopoda</i>). <b>c</b> CAFÃ-based estimates of gene family expansions and contractions. The green and red numbers indicate expanded and contracted gene families, respectively. Conserved gene families are indicated in blue in the pi charts. MRCA represents the most recent common ancestor. <b>d</b> Phylogenetic tree of <i>A. setaceus</i> and 12 other species based on a concatenated alignment of 1002 single-copy ortholog gene sets. The tree is rooted with <i>A. trichopoda</i> as the outgroup</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Further gene family analysis revealed that 898 gene families were expanded in <i>A. setaceus</i>, whereas 1093 gene families were lost from the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig4">4c</a>). In comparison with the close relative <i>A. officinalis</i>, which exhibits 558 expanded gene families and 1068 missing gene families, <i>A. setaceus</i> has gained more gene families. These expanded genes present diverse functions, such as binding, protein kinase activity, oxidoreductase activity, and transferase activity. As expected, a phylogenetic analysis showed that <i>A. setaceus</i> displayed a closer relationship to <i>A. officinalis</i> and phylogenetically diverged from the common ancestor ~9.66 million years ago (Mya), after the separation of Orchidaceae at 107.24 Mya (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig4">4d</a>).</p><p>Synteny analysis was performed for the <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i> genomes to understand the genome evolution of these two related species. High collinearity was observed between these two genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig5">5</a>). The relationships between the chromosomes of <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i> were illustrated based on the shared syntenic blocks. In general, each chromosome of <i>A. setaceus</i> corresponded to one chromosome of <i>A. officinalis</i>. For instance, <i>A. setaceus</i> Chr03 matched the sex chromosome of <i>A. officinalis</i> (Chr01, NC33794.1). In detail, 453 syntenic blocks containing more than three genes were identified from <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i>. The largest synteny block containing 329 genes was found between <i>A. setaceus</i> chromosome 03 and the <i>A. officinalis</i> sex chromosome (01, NC33794.1). Furthermore, the visualization of synteny blocks revealed frequent interchromosomal rearrangement events between the chromosomes of <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig5">5</a>). For example, most of the synteny blocks of <i>A. setaceus</i> Chr05 matched <i>A. officinalis</i> Chr05 (NC33798.1), but a few of them corresponded to <i>A. officinalis</i> Chr06 (NC33799.1), Chr07 (NC33800.1), Chr08 (NC33801.1), Chr09 (NC33802.1), or Chr10 (NC33803.1).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Macrosynteny analysis between the A. setaceus and A. officinalis chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Macrosynteny analysis between the <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i> chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="691" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Chr01âChr10 represent the chromosomes of <i>A. setaceus</i>, while NC33794.1âNC33803.1 correspond to the chromosomes of <i>A. officinalis</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome expansion in <i>A. setaceus</i>
</h3><p>To investigate the genome expansion in <i>A. setaceus</i>, we analyzed whole-genome duplication (WGD) events. 4DTv and Ks values were estimated on the basis of the paralogous gene pairs in collinear regions detected in <i>A. setaceus</i> and three other representative plant species, <i>P. dactylifera</i>, <i>P. equestris</i>, and <i>V. vinifera</i>. The distribution of Ks or 4DTv values in <i>A. setaceus</i> showed two distinct peaks at Ks values of ~0.58 (4DTv ~0.18) and ~1.00 (4DTv ~0.45; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig6">6a</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">4</a>). The first peak was shared by <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i>, and may correspond to the Asparagales-Î± event previously identified in the <i>A. officinalis</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Harkess, A. et al. The asparagus genome sheds light on the origin and evolution of a young Y chromosome. Nat. Commun. 8, 1279 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1340">11</a></sup>. The second is predicted to be derived from a more ancient WGD, which was also found in the <i>A. officinalis</i> genome based on Ks analysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM1">4</a>). Dot plots (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#Fig6">6b</a>) are presented for the paralogs that evolved from the two rounds of WGD events in <i>A. setaceus</i> genome (4â4 diagonal relationships).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="WGD analysis of the A. setaceus genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: WGD analysis of the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0271-y/MediaObjects/41438_2020_271_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="302" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b> 4DTv distribution in <i>A. setaceus</i> and other representative plant species. <b>b</b> Dot plots of paralogs in the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome, illustrating the two rounds of WGD events</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Positively selected genes in <i>A. setaceus</i>
</h3><p>To detect positively selected genes in <i>A. setaceus</i>, we evaluated the Ka/Ks ratios of genes with only one copy by using <i>A. setaceus</i> as a predetermined (foreground) branch and <i>A. officinalis</i> as a background branch. We detected 96 genes that have probably experienced positive selection. GO enrichment revealed that a majority of these genes were involved in ATP binding, nucleic acid binding, oxidoreductase activity, and oxidationâreduction processes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM2">10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Resistance R genes</h3><p>The <i>A. setaceus</i> genome included 76 resistance (R) genes with nucleotide-binding sites (NBSs). These genes constituted ~0.27% of all <i>A. setaceus</i> genes. Among these genes, 73 resided on the chromosomes, and three genes were located on unmapped scaffolds. These R genes belonged to five groups: TIR-NBS, CC-NBS-LRR, NBS-LRR, NBS, and CC-NBS. NBS-LRR was the largest group, including a total of 29 genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#MOESM2">12</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p><i>A. setaceus</i> is a popular ornamental plant species in many areas of the world. This plant species also has medicinal value. In addition, as a close relative of the important vegetable <i>A. officinalis</i>, comparative genetic and genomic studies on <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i> are helpful for investigating the mechanisms of disease resistance-related agricultural traits, and the origin and evolution of the sex chromosomes of <i>A. officinalis</i>. However, studies on this species are very limited. In particular, molecular-level studies are almost nonexistent. A genome sequence could greatly promote studies on this species and contribute to the comparative analysis of related <i>Asparagus</i> species.</p><p><i>A. setaceus</i> cv. âPyramidalisâ shows a very high level of heterozygosity (1.9%) and a high content of repeats (64.32%). The assembly of such a highly heterozygous and repetitive genome is a challenging task<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Nowak, M. D. et al. The draft genome of Primula veris yields insights into the molecular basis of heterostyly. Genome Biol. 16, 12 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1452">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Schatz, M. C., Witkowski, J. &amp; McCombie, W. R. Current challenges in de novo plant genome sequencing and assembly. Genome Res. 13, 243 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1455">23</a></sup>. Thus, we used a series of sequencing strategies, including the Nanopore, Illumina, 10Ã Genomics, and Hi-C sequencing platforms. We used Nanopore long reads for primary assembly, followed by assembly adjustment with highly accurate short reads. Then, 10Ã Genomics and Hi-C sequencing data were adopted for scaffold extension and superscaffold (chromosome) construction. By taking full advantage of these sequencing technologies, a chromosome-level genome assembly with high completeness and accuracy was obtained for <i>A. setaceus</i>. BUSCO assessment revealed that 90.0% of the complete BUSCOs could be found in the current assembled <i>A. setaceus</i> genome. This percentage was lower than those in <i>Osmanthus fragrans</i> (96.1%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Yang, X. et al. The chromosome-level quality genome provides insights into the evolution of the biosynthesis genes for aroma compounds of Osmanthus fragrans. Hortic. Res. 5, 72 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1468">24</a></sup> and <i>Brassica oleracea</i> (96.77%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Sun, D. et al. Draft genome sequence of cauliflower (Brassica oleracea L. var. botrytis) provides new insights into the C genome in Brassica species. Hortic. Res. 6, 82 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1476">25</a></sup>, but higher than those in the genomes of some other species, such as <i>Ginkgo biloba</i> (73.95%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Guan, R. et al. Draft genome of the living fossil Ginkgo biloba. Gigascience 5, 49 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1483">26</a></sup> and <i>A. officinalis</i> (88.2%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Harkess, A. et al. The asparagus genome sheds light on the origin and evolution of a young Y chromosome. Nat. Commun. 8, 1279 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1490">11</a></sup>. Considering the high level of heterozygosity and repetitiveness of the genome, the current version represents a high-quality genome assembly of <i>A. setaceus</i>.</p><p>To guarantee the accuracy of the genome annotation, we integrated various methods to annotate protein-coding genes and used an integrated pipeline to analyze repetitive sequences. A vast majority of the genes in the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome were functionally annotated. Repetitive sequences, mainly consisting of TEs, constitute a major fraction of eukaryotic genomes and play vital roles in genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wicker, T. et al. Impact of transposable elements on genome structure and evolution in bread wheat. Genome Biol. 19, 103 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1504">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Samoluk, S. S. et al. Heterochromatin evolution in Arachis investigated through genome-wide analysis of repetitive DNA. Planta 249, 1405â1415 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1507">28</a></sup>, chromosome rearrangement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Li, S. F. et al. Chromosome evolution in connection with repetitive sequences and epigenetics in plants. Genes 8, 290 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1511">29</a></sup>, and gene regulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="UzunoviÄ, J., Josephs, E. B., Stinchcombe, J. R. &amp; Wright, S. I. Transposable elements are important contributors to standing variation in gene expression in Capsella Grandiflora. Mol. Biol. Evol. 36, 1734â1745 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1515">30</a></sup>. Repetitive sequences occupied 65.59% of the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome assembly; this percentage is very similar to that in the <i>A. officinalis</i> genome (69%).</p><p>Comparative genomics analysis showed high synteny and colinearity between the genomes of <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i>. This observation is consistent with their close relationship. These two species diverged from their last common ancestor ~9.66 Mya. Thus, the transition from hermaphroditism to dioecy in <i>Asparagus</i> occurred &lt;9.66 Mya, and the sex chromosome of <i>A. officinalis</i> evolved from the ancestral autosome recently. The results agree with previous findings showing that the cytologically homomorphic X and Y sex chromosomes of <i>A. officinalis</i> are very young<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kubota, S., Konno, I. &amp; Kannos, A. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) explains interspecific crossability between the garden asparagus (A. officinalis) and other Asparagus species. Theor. Appl. Genet. 124, 345â354 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1545">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Ming, R., Bendahmane, A. &amp; Renner, S. S. Sex chromosomes in land plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 62, 485â514 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1548">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Telgmann-Rauber, A., Jamsari, A., Kinney, M. S., Pires, J. C. &amp; Jung, C. Genetic and physical maps around the sex-determining M-locus of the dioecious plant Asparagus. Mol. Genet. Genomics 278, 221â234 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1551">32</a></sup>. However, the accurate timing of the sex chromosome origin will require further analysis. We found that the <i>A. officinalis</i> sex chromosome and <i>A. setaceus</i> Chr03 shared a common ancestral chromosome. Further detailed comparative analyses of these two chromosomes would increase our knowledge of the evolution of the sex chromosome of <i>A. officinalis</i>.</p><p>It has been demonstrated that WGD events contribute greatly to the evolution of genomes and genes. Recent evidence has revealed that different plant lineages have experienced distinct WGD events. For example, the grape genome did not experience WGD after the Î³-event shared by eudicot plants that took place ~140 Mya, whereas the tea genome underwent two additional rounds of WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1568">33</a></sup>. The monocot species share a common WGD event, after which different species experienced lineage-specific WGD events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1345â1442 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1572">34</a></sup>. A previous study revealed that the <i>A. officinalis</i> genome underwent at least two ancient WGDs before the divergence of <i>A. officinalis</i> and other <i>Asparagus</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Harkess, A. et al. The asparagus genome sheds light on the origin and evolution of a young Y chromosome. Nat. Commun. 8, 1279 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1586">11</a></sup>. The high synteny and collinearity of the genomes of <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i> are in accord with the likely possibility that the two genomes experienced the same WGD events. In this study, the Ks and 4DTv distribution analysis revealed two distinct peaks, which likely correspond to the two rounds of WGD detected in <i>A. officinalis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Harkess, A. et al. The asparagus genome sheds light on the origin and evolution of a young Y chromosome. Nat. Commun. 8, 1279 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1598">11</a></sup>. The most ancient WGD event was not very clear in the <i>A. officinalis</i> genome based on the current analysis. This may be because of the gene loss process following the WGD events in the <i>A. officinalis</i> genome. The WGD events and subsequent diploidization have contributed greatly to the current genome structure of <i>A. setaceus</i>.</p><p>As a wild relative species of garden asparagus, <i>A. setaceus</i> is resistant to some common diseases caused by plant pathogens in <i>A. officinalis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Fukuda, T. et al. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) inferred from plastid petB intron and petD-rpoA intergenic spacer sequences. Plant Spec. Biol. 20, 121â132 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1620">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Bansal, R. K., Menzies, S. A. &amp; Broadhurst, P. G. Screening of Asparagus species for resistance to Stemphylium leaf spot. NZ J. Agric. Res. 29, 539â545 (1986)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1623">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Alberti, P. et al. Interspecific hybridization for Asparagus breeding. In Proc of the XLVIII Italian Society of Agricultural Genetics-SIFV-SIGA Joint Meeting (Lecce, Italy, 2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1626">15</a></sup>. In plants, R genes are usually involved in defense mechanisms against infections caused by a majority of specialized plant pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Meyers, B. C., Kaushik, S. &amp; Nandety, R. S. Evolving disease resistance genes. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 8, 129â134 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1630">35</a></sup>. Thus, examining the resistance genes of <i>A. setaceus</i> is helpful for the further molecular breeding of <i>A. officinalis</i>. Most of the extensively investigated plant R genes contain NBSs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Meyers, B. C., Kaushik, S. &amp; Nandety, R. S. Evolving disease resistance genes. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 8, 129â134 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1641">35</a></sup>. The <i>A. officinalis</i> genome contains 49 different NBS R genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Die, J. V., Castro, P., MillÃ¡n, T. &amp; Gil, J. Segmental and tandem duplication driving the recent NBS-LRR gene expansion in the asparagus genome. Genes 9, 568 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1648">36</a></sup>. In this study, we identified 76 non-redundant R genes in the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome, which was greater than the number in <i>A. officinalis</i>. Functional studies on these genes would improve our understanding of <i>A. setaceus</i> defense mechanisms and provide a basis for the molecular breeding of <i>A. officinalis</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>A chromosome-scale reference genome of <i>A. setaceus</i> (~710.15âMb) was generated by combining the Nanopore, Illumina, 10Ã Genomics, and Hi-C sequencing platforms. A total of 28,410 genes were identified. Among these genes, 90.22% were annotated. Repetitive sequences occupied 65.59% of the genome. The divergence between <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i> is estimated to have occurred ~9.66 Mya. Genome evolution analysis provided evidence supporting two rounds of WGD events. The identified genomic features of <i>A. setaceus</i>, including gene families, syntenic blocks, WGD events, and genome-specific genes, provide rich data for comparative genomic studies in plants, especially for studying species in the same genus. The divergence time and synteny analysis between <i>A. setaceus</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i> will contribute to studies on the evolution of the sex chromosome of <i>A. officinalis</i> and the <i>Asparagus</i> genus.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Molecular karyotype analysis of <i>A. setaceus</i>
</h3><p>A plant of <i>A. setaceus</i> cv. âPyramidalisâ cultivated in the glasshouse of Henan Normal University was used in this study. The preparation of mitotic metaphase spreads, fluorescence in situ hybridization (FISH), and molecular karyotype analysis were performed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, S. F. et al. The landscape of transposable elements and satellite DNAs in the genome of a dioecious plant spinach (Spinacia oleracea L.). Mol. DNA 10, 3 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1717">37</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Genome sequencing</h3><p>Total DNA was isolated from young fascicled cladodes and stems by using the CTAB method to construct Nanopore and Illumina libraries. For each Nanopore library, the genomic DNA was fractionated (10â50âkb) with BluePippin (Sage Science, Beverly, MA), repaired, A tailed, adaptor ligated, and used for library construction in accordance with the Nanopore library construction protocol. A total of 67 libraries were generated and sequenced on the GridION X5 sequencer platform (Oxford Nanopore Technologies, UK) at the Nextomics Biosciences Company (Wuhan, China).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">10Ã Genomics linked read sequencing</h3><p>High-molecular weight DNA extraction, indexing, and barcoding were performed in accordance with the standard protocols provided by 10Ã Genomics. Approximately 1âng of sample DNA was used for GEM generation, and 16âbp barcodes were used for the labeling of droplets. After the GEM reactions were thermally amplified, the droplets were fractured, and the intermediate DNA library was purified. Then, the DNA was sheared into 500âbp fragments to construct libraries. Sequencing was performed by using an Illumina HiSeq X Ten sequencer to generate linked reads. The long DNA molecules contained many short reads sharing the same barcode.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Hi-C sequencing</h3><p>Hi-C sequencing data were generated to obtain physical scaffolds for genome assembly as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Belton, J. M. et al. Hi-C: a comprehensive technique to capture the conformation of genomes. Methods 58, 268â276 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1745">38</a></sup>. Briefly, fresh spears were harvested, cut into small sections, and immersed in 2% formaldehyde for 15âmin for crosslinking. Thereafter, the materials were crushed into a fine powder and used for the isolation of nuclei. The isolated nuclei were purified, digested with <i>Dpn</i> II, blunt-end-repaired, and tagged with biotin. Then, the DNA was religated with the T4 DNA ligation enzyme. After proteinase K digestion and the reversion of formaldehyde crosslinking, biotin-containing DNA fragments were captured and used for the construction of the Hi-C library. The final libraries were sequenced by using an Illumina HiSeq X ten sequencer.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">RNA-seq</h3><p>Total RNA was isolated separately from leaves, stems, and flowers of the same <i>A. setaceus</i> individual by using a QIAGEN RNeasy plant mini kit (QIAGEN, Hilden, Germany). Thereafter, RNA-seq libraries were constructed with a TruSeq RNA library preparation kit (Illumina), and PE150 sequencing was carried out on the HiSeq X ten platform. A total of 7.8âGb, 7.1âGb, and 8.5âGb of sequences were generated from the three sample types. In addition, full-length transcriptome sequencing was conducted for mixed samples by using the PacBio Sequel platform, obtaining an additional 14âGb of data.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Heterozygosity estimation</h3><p>The heterozygosity of <i>A. setaceus</i> was estimated via K-mer frequency analysis by using Illumina sequencing data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="MarÃ§ais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting for occurrences K-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764â760 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1775">39</a></sup> in accordance with previously described methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Yang, X. et al. The chromosome-level quality genome provides insights into the evolution of the biosynthesis genes for aroma compounds of Osmanthus fragrans. Hortic. Res. 5, 72 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1779">24</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Genome assembly</h3><p>Oxford Nanopore sequencing data were filtered (mean_qscoreâ&gt;â7) and then employed for genome assembly by using the complete Canu pipeline with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1791">40</a></sup>. The paired-end Illumina reads were mapped to the assembly to improve its accuracy for base-pair correction with BWA MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1795">41</a></sup> and Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1799">42</a></sup>.</p><p>The scaffolds were extended with 10Ã Genomics data by using ARKS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Coombe, L. et al. ARKS: chromosome-scale scaffolding of human genome drafts with linked read kmers. BMC Bioinform. 19, 234 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1806">43</a></sup> with the following parameters: â-c 5 -j 0.55 -m 50-10000 -k 30 -r 0.05 -e 3000 -z 500 -d 0â, and LINKS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Warren, R. L. et al. LINKS: scalable, alignment-free scaffolding of draft genomes with long reads. Gigascience 4, 35 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1810">44</a></sup> with the following parameters: â-l 5 -a 0.9 -z 500â. Thereafter, redundancy in the assembly was removed using redundans 0-13c<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Pryszcz, L. P. &amp; Gabaldon, T. Redundans: an assembly pipeline for highly heterozygous genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e113 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1814">45</a></sup> with the following parameters: ââidentity 0.7 âoverlap 0.7â.</p><p>The Hi-C sequencing data were used for the scaffolding of the preliminary assemblies and to increase the contiguity of the assembly at the chromosome level. The cleaned paired-end reads generated by the Illumina HiSeq platform from the Hi-C library were aligned to the assemblies by using Bowtie2 (version 2.3.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357â359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1821">46</a></sup>. After the map position and orientation of the unique mapped reads were considered, the validated read pairs were filtered. Then, LACHESIS software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interaction. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119â1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1825">47</a></sup>, which applies a hierarchical agglomerative clustering strategy, was used for chromosome-level scaffolding by clustering, ordering, and orienting the previous assemblies based on genomic proximity information between Hi-C read pairs. Finally, the adjacent anchored scaffolds were connected using 100âbp Ns to form ten superscaffolds corresponding to ten chromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Gene and repetitive sequence annotation</h3><p>Protein-coding genes were identified using strategies that combined de novo gene prediction, experimental evidence obtained from transcriptomic data, and homology-based methods. For homology prediction, GeMoMa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Keilwagen, J. et al. Using intron position conservation for homology-based gene prediction. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e89 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1837">48</a></sup> was used with a protein sequence from <i>A. officinalis</i>, a relative of <i>A. setaceus</i>. For RNA-seq-based prediction, PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Campbell, M. A., Hass, B. J., Hamilton, J. P., Mount, S. M. &amp; Buell, C. R. Comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in rice and comparative analyses with Arabidopsis. BMC Genomics 7, 327 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1847">49</a></sup> was used on the basis of the assembled RNA-seq unigenes. Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Stanke, M., SchÃ¶ffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinform. 7, 62 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1851">50</a></sup> was used for de novo prediction. Then, genes identified by these methods were integrated with EVM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1856">51</a></sup>. Then, the sequences of the predicted genes were searched against the commonly used SwissProt, GO, KEGG, KOG, Nr, and InterPro databases for annotation.</p><p>For the annotation of noncoding RNAs, tRNAscan-SE software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1863">52</a></sup> was used to predict the tRNAs with eukaryotic parameters. miRNAs, rRNAs, and snRNAs were detected using Infernal cmscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Nawrocki, E. P. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics 29, 2933â2935 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1867">53</a></sup> to search the Rfam database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Griffiths-Jones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D121âD124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1871">54</a></sup>. The rRNAs and the corresponding subunits were annotated with RNAmmer v1.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Lagesen, K. et al. RNAmmer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, 3100â3108 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1875">55</a></sup>.</p><p>Repeat annotation was conducted using RepeatMasker based on a custom library produced using de novo-based and homology-based strategies. The de novo prediction of repeats was carried out by using RepeatModeler. A homology-based detection procedure was performed using a conserved BLASTN search in Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mol. DNA 6, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0271-y#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d14961e1882">56</a></sup>. The consensus families generated by RepeatModeler and repeat sequences with similarity in Repbase were merged as a database to analyze the <i>A. setaceus</i> genome by using RepeatMasker.
